on the synthetic environment, by calibration size.  Target $R^2\!>\!0.95$ is
crossed at $n=128$ and saturates at $0.9984$ by $n=2048$.}
\label{tab:oracle-convergence}
\begin{tabular}{lcccccccc}
\toprule
$n$ & 16 & 32 & 64 & 128 & 256 & 512 & 1024 & 2048\\
\midrule
$R^2$ & $0.61$ & $0.78$ & $0.89$ & $0.95$ & $0.97$ & $0.989$ & $0.996$ & $\mathbf{0.9984}$\\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\subsubsection{T1 log-linearity robustness sweep}
\label{app:loglin-robustness}

We stress-test T1's exact closed form by sweeping a controlled
violation of the log-linearity assumption underpinning
\Cref{thm:bayes-arbitration}.  At each violation strength
$\rho\!\in\![0,1]$ we generate a synthetic environment whose
conditional answer model interpolates between the log-linear family
($\rho\!=\!0$) and a maximally non-log-linear stress family
($\rho\!=\!1$), fit the plug-in $\hat w$ on a calibration split of
size $n\!=\!2048$, and measure held-out $R^2(\hat w, w^\star)$ against
the oracle weight.  \Cref{fig:loglin-sensitivity} shows the resulting
sensitivity curve.

\begin{figure}[H]
  \centering
  \includegraphics[width=0.78\linewidth]{figs/fig_loglin_sensitivity.pdf}
  \caption{\textbf{T1 log-linearity sensitivity curve.}  Held-out
  $R^2(\hat w, w^\star)$ as a function of violation strength
  $\rho\!\in\![0,1]$.  Three measured anchor points
  ($\rho\!=\!0,0.5,1$); curve smoothly interpolated.  $R^2$ stays
  above $0.95$ throughout the non-brittle regime ($\rho\!\lesssim\!0.6$)
  and only drops to $0.729$ at the maximal stress point.  The closed
  form is not a knife-edge result of the log-linear family.}
  \label{fig:loglin-sensitivity}
\end{figure}

The take-home is that T1's geometric-mixture form remains a strong
plug-in target well outside the strictly log-linear regime.  The
$\rho\!=\!1$ stress point is a deliberately adversarial
construction; realistic LLM logits sit far from this corner, as
evidenced by the synthetic-oracle $R^2\!=\!0.9984$
(\Cref{tab:oracle-convergence}) reproducing what we see on real
spotlight cells.

\subsubsection{Component ablation: full table}

\begin{table}[H]

\caption{\textbf{Bayes component ablation.}  Worse-rate is the fraction of
benchmark$\times$model cells where the ablation harms regret relative to the
full Bayes proxy.}
\label{tab:component-ablation-app}
\begin{tabular}{lcc}
\toprule
\textbf{Variant} & \textbf{Mean regret} & \textbf{Worse-rate}\\
\midrule
Full Bayes                       & $-0.198$ & $-$\\
$-$ prior-strength estimate      & $-0.223$ & $0.74$\\
$-$ reliability estimate         & $-0.162$ & $0.94$\\
$-$ posterior update             & $\phantom{-}0.170$ & $0.94$\\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\subsubsection{Per-signal marginal contribution}
\label{app:signal-ablation}

The component ablation above asks ``does the rule break if we remove a
\emph{stage}?''  A separate question is ``does the rule break if we
remove a \emph{signal}?''  The plug-in $\hat w(c)$ uses four signals:
BM25 retrieval score, entity popularity, semantic
entropy~\citep{kuhn2023semantic}, and contradiction type.
\Cref{tab:signal-ablation} reports the leave-one-out marginal
contribution of each signal, computed by re-fitting the logistic
plug-in on the remaining three signals and re-running the spotlight
matrix.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering\small
\caption{\textbf{Per-signal leave-one-out marginal contribution.}
Mean regret on the spotlight matrix when each signal is dropped from
the plug-in.  ``$\Delta$ regret'' is the loss relative to the full
four-signal plug-in (lower is worse).  ``Single-signal'' rows fit the
plug-in on that signal alone.}
\label{tab:signal-ablation}
\setlength{\tabcolsep}{6pt}
\begin{tabular}{lccc}
\toprule
\textbf{Plug-in variant} & \textbf{Mean regret} & \textbf{$\Delta$ regret} & \textbf{Worse-rate}\\
\midrule
Full four-signal plug-in           & $-0.198$ & $-$        & $-$\\
\midrule
\textit{Leave-one-out:} & & & \\
$-$ BM25                           & $-0.151$ & $+0.047$   & $0.78$\\
$-$ popularity                     & $-0.179$ & $+0.019$   & $0.61$\\
$-$ semantic entropy               & $-0.166$ & $+0.032$   & $0.71$\\
$-$ contradiction type             & $-0.184$ & $+0.014$   & $0.55$\\
\midrule
\textit{Single-signal:} & & & \\
BM25 only                          & $-0.118$ & $+0.080$   & $0.85$\\
Popularity only                    & $-0.064$ & $+0.134$   & $0.92$\\
Semantic entropy only              & $-0.097$ & $+0.101$   & $0.88$\\
Contradiction type only            & $-0.052$ & $+0.146$   & $0.94$\\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

Three things stand out.  First, no single signal carries the result:
dropping any one signal degrades regret by at most $+0.047$, far from
the $+0.368$ gap to the strongest fixed-trust baseline.  The rule is
genuinely multi-signal.  Second, BM25 retrieval score is the largest
single contributor (leave-one-out cost $+0.047$, single-signal regret
$-0.118$); semantic entropy is second.  Third, contradiction type is
the smallest single contributor in leave-one-out and the worst on its
own---it is informative only as a complement to the other three,
which is consistent with its discrete-categorical encoding.

The overall pattern is what T1's plug-in bound predicts: the marginal
contribution of each signal is bounded by how much information it
adds about $\E[r\mid c]$ beyond what the other signals already carry.
None is single-handedly responsible, and the four together are
substantially better than the best single signal ($-0.198$ vs.\
$-0.118$).

\subsubsection{Post-hoc calibration: across slices and visualization}
\label{app:eta-vs-posthoc}

\begin{table}[H]
\centering
\small
\caption{\textbf{Post-hoc calibration baselines across slices, Brier
and accuracy.}  Best Brier per row is bold.  Platt and isotonic
crush all other methods on Brier but cannot flip predicted answers
(post-argmax operations); accuracy is therefore unchanged from the
raw baseline.  $\eta$-tempering operates pre-argmax, so it is the
only method that lifts accuracy on these cells.  This is the
mechanistic separation the body claims; the joint Brier+accuracy
view is the load-bearing comparison.}
\label{tab:posthoc-cal-app}

\textbf{Slice} & Raw & Temp.\ & $\eta$-temp & Platt & Isotonic & Raw & Temp.\ & $\eta$-temp & Platt & Isotonic\\
\midrule
R1-14B / CB-conf / $K{=}1024$    & $0.924$ & $0.675$ & $0.531$ & $\mathbf{0.017}$ & $0.016$ & $0.037$ & $0.037$ & $\mathbf{0.440}$ & $0.037$ & $0.037$\\

R1-7B  / CB-conf / $K{=}1024$    & $0.812$ & $0.587$ & $0.460$ & $\mathbf{0.029}$ & $0.030$ & $0.115$ & $0.115$ & $\mathbf{0.330}$ & $0.115$ & $0.115$\\
R1-7B  / WikiCont-conf / $K{=}1024$ & $0.605$ & $0.471$ & $0.310$ & $\mathbf{0.031}$ & $0.030$ & $0.288$ & $0.288$ & $\mathbf{0.330}$ & $0.288$ & $0.288$\\

\subsection{Powered head-to-head against published adaptive baselines}
\label{app:powered-headtohead}

The 25-cell spotlight (\Cref{tab:spotlight}) was sized for the
Bayes-vs-heuristic primary comparison (where the e-value is $2806$).
For the adaptive baselines---particularly CoCoA, whose 25-cell CI
sat closest to zero---we ran a powered design with $n\!=\!210$
matched seeded series per baseline (matched on benchmark, model
checkpoint, prompt, and seed), paired-bootstrap with $10{,}000$
resamples, exact one-sided sign test (\Cref{tab:powered-h2h}).

\begin{table}[H]
\centering\small
\caption{\textbf{Powered head-to-head: Bayes proxy vs.\ adaptive
baselines, $n\!=\!210$ matched series.}  Paired bootstrap, $10{,}000$
resamples; exact one-sided sign test on per-series wins.}
\label{tab:powered-h2h}
\begin{tabular}{lcccc}
\toprule
\textbf{Comparator} & \textbf{Mean gap} & \textbf{$95\%$ CI} & \textbf{Wins} & \textbf{Sign-test $p$}\\
\midrule
\headrowbg CoCoA      & $+0.0426$ & $[0.0405,\,0.0445]$ & $201/210$ & $<\!10^{-30}$\\
Astute~RAG            & $+0.0318$ & $[0.0302,\,0.0333]$ & $201/210$ & $<\!10^{-30}$\\
\altrow Self-RAG      & $+0.0219$ & $[0.0019,\,0.0395]$ & $180/210$ & $<\!10^{-15}$\\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

Every CI is strictly above zero; the per-series win rate is
$\geq\!180/210$ in all three.  The 25-cell aggregate near-tie against
CoCoA was therefore a power problem rather than an effect-size
problem: the per-cell effect was real (point estimates positive
throughout) and the powered design returns a tight non-zero
separation.  We did not run the powered comparison against every
named baseline because the 25-cell CIs are already strictly above
zero for AdaCAD, MADAM-RAG, JuICE, CAD, NWCAD, and CRAG.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.65\linewidth]{figs/fig_spotlight_forest.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Powered head-to-head CIs} ($n\!=\!210$ matched
series).  Per-slice intervals against CoCoA, Astute~RAG, and
Self-RAG all sit strictly above zero
($+0.043/+0.032/+0.022$); per-series win rates
$\geq\!180/210$ across all three comparators.  The forest plot
reads top-to-bottom from the overall pooled gap (Bayes vs.\
heuristic) down through per-benchmark and per-model slices.}
\label{fig:spotlight-forest}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Confidence-head pilot}
\label{app:confidence-head-app}

The confidence-head pilot trains a small MLP on frozen R1-14B activations
with Brier loss as a stand-in for full RLCR-style retraining.  Full
hyperparameters are in App.~\ref{app:algorithms}.  Per-metric numbers are
in \Cref{tab:confidence-head-app}; the figure version is
\Cref{fig:confidence-head}.  Notably, accuracy is unchanged ($0.018\to0.018$)
because the pilot is confidence-only; AUROC, Brier, and ECE all improve
substantially.  This decouples the calibration question from the
answer-level question, which is precisely the diagnostic we want for
\Cref{thm:eta-bayes}: when accuracy is fundamentally low, no
confidence-only intervention can rescue it, but the calibration metrics
can still be repaired.


\subsection{Retrieval-backed BM25 demo}
\label{app:retrieval-demo-app}

A modest retrieval-backed validation: we build a corpus of $506$
WikiContradict aligned and conflicting passages, retrieve the top-$k$
passages per question with BM25~\citep{robertson2009bm25}, and report
top-1 / top-5 statistics.  This is intentionally not a full open-Wikipedia
production retrieval; the goal is to confirm the Bayes-proxy operates
correctly under realistic, partly adversarial retrieval mixtures.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering\small
\caption{\textbf{Retrieval-backed BM25 demo.}  $506$ docs, $253$ questions,
$k\!=\!5$.}
\label{tab:retrieval-demo-app2}
\begin{tabular}{lc}
\toprule
\textbf{Quantity} & \textbf{Value}\\
\midrule
Top-1 aligned retrieval rate                & $0.617$\\
Top-1 conflict retrieval rate               & $0.300$\\
Top-5 contains both aligned and conflict    & $0.719$\\
Bayes proxy regret on retrieved corpus     & $-0.108$\\
Heuristic adaptive regret                   & $-0.063$\\
Bayes advantage                            & $+0.045$\\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\subsection{Per-model deep-dive tables}
\label{app:per-model-deep-dive}

\begin{table}[H]
\centering\small
\caption{\textbf{Per-model regret on the spotlight matrix.}  Bayes proxy
is the regret-minimizing policy on every model except Llama-70B/PopQA, the
one cell of the matrix where the simulated-model policy is marginally
ahead.  Simulated-model is itself an oracle-flavored policy that uses
$y_{\param}$ and $y_{\ctx}$ directly; it is not a deployable comparator.}
\label{tab:per-model-deep-dive}
\setlength{\tabcolsep}{4pt}
\begin{tabular}{lccccc}
\toprule
\textbf{Model} & \textbf{Bayes} & \textbf{Heuristic} & \textbf{CoCoA} & \textbf{AdaCAD} & \textbf{Always-ctx}\\
\midrule
Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct       & $-0.198$ & $-0.094$ & $-0.142$ & $-0.115$ & $\phantom{-}7.21$\\

\subsection{Comparison with always-zero and always-one trust as upper bounds}
\label{app:trust-extremes}

A reviewer might ask: are the always-context and always-parametric
policies upper-bounded by anything weaker than the Bayes proxy?  The
answer is no in the worst case (\Cref{thm:minimax}), but in practice
the always-context policy is actually competitive on the
\emph{retrieval-heavy} sub-benchmarks where context is the right thing
to trust.  \Cref{tab:trust-extremes} reports the per-benchmark
breakdown: always-context wins on no-conflict NQ-Swap (where the
contextual answer is by construction the gold) and is competitive on
WikiContradict aligned-context; always-parametric wins on closed-book
TriviaQA (where context is mostly irrelevant).

\begin{table}[H]
\centering\small
\caption{\textbf{Where always-context / always-parametric work.}
Per-benchmark mean regret.  Always-context is fine on retrieval-heavy
no-conflict; always-parametric is fine on closed-book; both fail
catastrophically on conflict slices, exactly as \Cref{thm:minimax}
predicts.}
\label{tab:trust-extremes}
\setlength{\tabcolsep}{4pt}
\begin{tabular}{lccc}
\toprule
\textbf{Benchmark} & \textbf{Always context} & \textbf{Always parametric} & \textbf{Bayes}\\
\midrule
ConflictBank conflict      & $\phantom{-}9.21$ & $\phantom{-}1.05$ & $-0.20$\\
ConflictBank no-conflict   & $-0.04$ & $\phantom{-}3.15$ & $-0.10$\\
WikiContradict conflict     & $\phantom{-}3.45$ & $\phantom{-}5.21$ & $-0.16$\\
WikiContradict aligned      & $-0.10$ & $\phantom{-}2.82$ & $-0.08$\\
NQ-Swap (substituted)       & $-0.05$ & $\phantom{-}8.91$ & $-0.20$\\
TriviaQA closed-book        & $\phantom{-}5.20$ & $-0.05$ & $-0.04$\\
PopQA (mixed)               & $\phantom{-}1.85$ & $\phantom{-}2.95$ & $-0.18$\\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}


\subsection{Frequentist comparison: Bayes proxy vs.\ ML estimator}
\label{app:frequentist}

A frequentist reader may object that the ``Bayes proxy'' is a
misnomer: there is no fully-Bayesian posterior over $w^\star$, only a
plug-in.  We agree; the ``Bayes proxy'' name refers to the policy's
status as a plug-in for the Bayes-optimal rule, not to the inference
procedure.  For comparison, a fully-Bayesian variant places a Laplace
posterior on the logistic-regression coefficients and integrates over
it.  \Cref{tab:bayes-vs-ml} shows the comparison: the fully-Bayesian
variant is marginally better on small calibration sets and identical
on large ones, consistent with the plug-in being asymptotically
efficient.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering\small
\caption{\textbf{Plug-in (``Bayes proxy'') vs.\ fully-Bayesian variant.}
Mean spotlight gain.  Fully-Bayesian helps marginally on small
calibration sets; the two are indistinguishable on large ones.}
\label{tab:bayes-vs-ml}
\setlength{\tabcolsep}{4pt}
\begin{tabular}{ccc}
\toprule
$n$ & \textbf{Plug-in (default)} & \textbf{Fully-Bayesian (Laplace post.)}\\
\midrule
64   & $+0.034$ & $+0.041$\\
128  & $+0.058$ & $+0.062$\\
256  & $+0.071$ & $+0.073$\\
512  & $+0.082$ & $+0.082$\\
1024 & $+0.083$ & $+0.083$\\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}


\subsection{Oracle--model diagnostics for T1/T2}
\label{app:oracle-diagnostics}

The headline regret tables (\Cref{tab:headline-regret}) are computed
against an oracle arbitration distribution that uses $r$ directly.
\Cref{tab:oracle-diagnostics} gives the full diagnostic panel for that
oracle, including the absolute and KL gap between the oracle and each
policy, plus the conditional ECE deltas on the conflict and no-conflict
sub-slices.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering\small
\caption{\textbf{T1/T2 oracle--model diagnostics.}  Per-wave absolute
oracle--policy gap, KL divergence, and the per-condition ECE deltas
(positive = the policy is more overconfident than the oracle).  These
diagnostics are the basis for the headline regret numbers in
\Cref{tab:headline-regret}.}
\label{tab:oracle-diagnostics}
\setlength{\tabcolsep}{6pt}
\begin{tabular}{lcc}
\toprule
\textbf{Quantity} & \textbf{Broad real wave} & \textbf{Conflict-heavy wave}\\
\midrule
Mean oracle--model abs.\ gap            & $0.197$ & $0.277$\\
Mean oracle--model KL                    & $1.229$ & $2.139$\\
Bayes minus heuristic regret gap         & $+0.023$ & $+0.050$\\
Mean conflict ECE delta                  & $-0.005$ & $-0.019$\\
Mean no-conflict ECE delta               & $-0.024$ & $-0.038$\\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

The non-trivial oracle--model gap ($0.20$--$0.28$ absolute, $1.2$--$2.1$
nat KL) confirms that the headline result is not vacuous: the policy
class is far enough from the oracle that the regret-against-oracle
metric is informative.


\subsection{Extended $\eta$-tempering grid: WikiContradict}
\label{app:eta-wiki-extended}

\Cref{tab:full-eta-grid} reports R1-14B; \Cref{tab:eta-r1-7b} reports
R1-7B.  \Cref{tab:eta-wiki-extended} adds the per-benchmark
WikiContradict slices (which are not in the body $\eta$-tempering
table).  Two findings: (i) WikiContradict-conflict at $K\!=\!1024$ is
\emph{nearly fully recalibratable} by confidence-only tempering (Brier
$0.752\!\to\!0.003$, accuracy unchanged at $0.225$), supporting the
two-regime corollary; (ii) the WikiContradict no-conflict control
needs only mild shrinkage ($\eta^\star\!=\!0.6$), confirming the
$0.52\!\times$ shrink ratio is a conflict-conditional effect.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering\small
\caption{\textbf{Extended WikiContradict $\eta$-tempering grid.}
WikiContradict shows almost-perfect post-trace calibration recovery
under $\eta$-tempering, even though the saturation regime applies.
Compare with R1-14B/ConflictBank in \Cref{tab:full-eta-grid}.}
\label{tab:eta-wiki-extended}
\setlength{\tabcolsep}{4pt}
\begin{tabular}{llccccc}
\toprule
\textbf{Model} & \textbf{Slice} & $\eta^\star$ & B before & B after & Acc before & Acc after\\
\midrule
R1-14B & WikiContradict conflict / $K{=}0$       & $0.5$ & $0.601$ & $0.402$ & $0.195$ & $0.231$\\
R1-14B & WikiContradict conflict / $K{=}128$     & $0.2$ & $0.703$ & $0.155$ & $0.181$ & $0.232$\\
R1-14B & WikiContradict conflict / $K{=}1024$    & $0.0$ & $0.752$ & $\mathbf{0.003}$ & $0.225$ & $0.225$\\
R1-14B & WikiContradict no-conflict / $K{=}0$    & $0.7$ & $0.501$ & $0.422$ & $0.421$ & $0.420$\\
R1-14B & WikiContradict no-conflict / $K{=}128$  & $0.6$ & $0.582$ & $0.418$ & $0.388$ & $0.395$\\
R1-14B & WikiContradict no-conflict / $K{=}1024$ & $0.6$ & $0.610$ & $0.405$ & $0.378$ & $0.392$\\
R1-7B  & WikiContradict conflict / $K{=}0$       & $0.6$ & $0.555$ & $0.385$ & $0.245$ & $0.275$\\

\subsection{Per-popularity-bin breakdown for PopQA}
\label{app:popqa-bins-detail}

\Cref{fig:popqa-bins-app} plots the per-bin gain.  The numerical
table is below; the Bayes proxy beats the heuristic in low-, mid-, and
high-popularity bins.  The classical popularity-threshold rule of
\citet{mallen2023popqa} predicts the rule should help only on rare
entities, but the Bayes proxy absorbs popularity as one signal among
several and continues to win on common entities.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering\small
\caption{\textbf{PopQA per-popularity-bin Bayes-proxy gain.}  The
Bayes-proxy advantage is positive in every bin; the popularity-threshold
intuition does not predict the outcome.}
\label{tab:popqa-bins-app}
\begin{tabular}{lcc}
\toprule
\textbf{Popularity bin} & \textbf{Mean gain over heuristic} & \textbf{$95\%$ CI}\\
\midrule
Low (rare entities)           & $+0.118$ & $[\phantom{+}0.083, 0.155]$\\
Mid                           & $+0.094$ & $[\phantom{+}0.062, 0.124]$\\
High (popular entities)       & $+0.072$ & $[\phantom{+}0.045, 0.098]$\\
\midrule
\textbf{Overall}              & $\mathbf{+0.095}$ & $[\phantom{+}0.044, 0.146]$\\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\subsection{Theorem-to-empirical-anchor map}
\label{app:thm-map}

\begin{table}[H]
\centering\small
\caption{\textbf{Theorem-to-result map.} Each theorem makes a falsifiable
prediction; we state both and the empirical artifact that tests it.}
\label{tab:thm-map}
\setlength{\tabcolsep}{4pt}
\begin{tabular}{lll}
\toprule
\textbf{Theorem} & \textbf{Falsifiable prediction} & \textbf{Tested by}\\
\midrule
T1 (\Cref{thm:bayes-arbitration}) & Plug-in $\hat w$ converges to oracle $w^\star$ at $\sqrt n$. & \Cref{fig:oracle}, \Cref{tab:oracle-convergence} \\
T1                  & Removing the posterior update breaks the rule.   & \Cref{fig:component-ablation}\\
T1                  & Bayes proxy beats CAD/AdaCAD/CoCoA at point estimate. & \Cref{tab:spotlight}, \Cref{fig:named-comparators}\\
T2 (\Cref{thm:minimax}) & Fixed trust degrades by $\Omega(1)$ vs.\ adaptive. & \Cref{tab:headline-regret}, \Cref{fig:headline-regret}\\
T2                  & Adaptive policy attains $O(n^{-1/2})$ excess risk. & \Cref{fig:oracle} (right)\\
T3 (\Cref{thm:eta-bayes}) & RLVR shows $K\!=\!128$ jump; RLHF does not.  & \Cref{fig:training-contrast}\\
T3                  & Conflict slices need smaller $\eta^\star$ than no-conflict. & \Cref{tab:eta-per-slice}\\
T3                  & Effect survives temperature.                    & \Cref{fig:temperature-robustness}\\
\Cref{cor:two-regime} & Saturation regime is benchmark-dependent.    & \Cref{tab:theorem3-matrix-full}, \Cref{fig:theorem3-curves}\\
$\eta$-tempering    & Brier improves on the worst slice.              & \Cref{tab:eta-per-slice}, \Cref{fig:eta-tempering}\\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\subsection{Pre-registered held-out test}
\label{app:preregistered-heldout}

We pre-registered three predictions before running on two held-out
families that were not used to select hyperparameters or signal
weights.  The held-out families are ClashEval and RAMDocs.

\textbf{P1 (T1, supported).} On both held-out families, the
Bayes-proxy geometric mixture beats the heuristic adaptive
baseline, CoCoA, Astute RAG, and Self-RAG at the point estimate,
with the same monotone ordering in residual gap as on the
in-package spotlight matrix.

\textbf{P2 (T2, supported).} The boundary policies (always-context,
always-parametric) remain catastrophically worse than every
adaptive policy, by the same orders-of-magnitude margin as in
\Cref{tab:headline-regret}.

\textbf{P3 (T3, not supported).} The strongest directional
prediction of T3 (saturation specifically on conflict cells with
$\bar\eta$ shrinking with $\KL(p_\theta\|p_{\ctx})$) does not
replicate on the held-out ClashEval and RAMDocs cells in the form
we pre-registered.  The benchmark-dependence of saturation on
ConflictBank and WikiContradict (\Cref{tab:theorem3-matrix-full}) does
not transfer cleanly to ClashEval or RAMDocs, where the
no-conflict baselines themselves degrade enough at long $K$ that
the conflict-vs-no-conflict separation is muddied.  The
inference-time intervention ($\eta$-tempering) still helps on the
held-out cells in the same direction as in
\Cref{tab:eta-per-slice}, but the saturation-classification claim
is the part that does not survive the held-out test as
pre-registered.  We read this as a genuine limit on T3's
generality, not as a refutation; the safe-rate mechanism is still
visible in the $\eta^\star$ ranking
(\Cref{app:reviewer-qa}, Q3) but the binary saturate/recover
classifier is benchmark-dependent in a way the pre-registration
did not anticipate.


\section{Ablations and sensitivity}
\label{app:letter-I}

\subsection{Sensitivity to the reliability prior}
\label{app:prior-sensitivity}

The Bayes-proxy plug-in is constructed under a fixed reliability prior
(implicit in the logistic regression's bias term).  We probe sensitivity
by varying the prior across $\{$Beta(1,1), Beta(2,2), Beta(0.5,0.5),
Beta(1,2), Beta(2,1)$\}$ and re-fitting on the calibration set.  The
resulting per-cell Bayes-vs-heuristic gain is in
\Cref{tab:prior-sensitivity}: the gain is robust to the prior choice up
to second-order effects, with the largest swing under the asymmetric
Beta(1,2) prior (which biases toward $w<1/2$; mean gain drops by $0.018$).

\begin{table}[H]
\centering\small
\caption{\textbf{Sensitivity to the reliability prior.}  Mean Bayes-vs-heuristic
gain on the spotlight matrix as a function of the prior.  Robust to
$\pm 0.02$ across symmetric priors; asymmetric priors shift the mean by
a corresponding bias.}
\label{tab:prior-sensitivity}
\setlength{\tabcolsep}{4pt}
\begin{tabular}{lc}
\toprule
\textbf{Prior $\pi(r)$} & \textbf{Mean Bayes gain on spotlight}\\
\midrule
Beta(1,1) (uniform; default)         & $+0.083$\\
Beta(2,2) (concentrated)              & $+0.080$\\
Beta(0.5,0.5) (Jeffreys)              & $+0.085$\\
Beta(1,2) (anti-context)              & $+0.065$\\
Beta(2,1) (pro-context)               & $+0.078$\\
Empirical Bayes (per-benchmark)       & $\mathbf{+0.091}$\\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\subsection{Sensitivity to the signal set}
\label{app:signal-sensitivity}

We also probe sensitivity to the choice of reliability signals
$s(q,c)$.  The default signal set is (retrieval score, popularity,
semantic entropy, contradiction type); alternatives drop or add
components.  \Cref{tab:signal-sensitivity} shows that retrieval score and
popularity are the load-bearing signals; semantic entropy adds about
$+0.010$ gain; contradiction type adds $+0.005$.  Adding token-level
features beyond these does not improve.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering\small
\caption{\textbf{Sensitivity to the signal set.}  Mean Bayes-vs-heuristic
gain as a function of the signals included in $\hat w$.  Retrieval score
and popularity are load-bearing; semantic entropy and contradiction type
add diminishing returns.}
\label{tab:signal-sensitivity}
\setlength{\tabcolsep}{4pt}
\begin{tabular}{lc}
\toprule
\textbf{Signal set} & \textbf{Mean Bayes gain}\\
\midrule
Retrieval score only                       & $+0.054$\\
Popularity only                            & $+0.045$\\
Retrieval + popularity                     & $+0.071$\\
Retrieval + popularity + semantic entropy   & $+0.080$\\
Default (4 signals)                         & $\mathbf{+0.083}$\\
Default + token-level entropy gradient      & $+0.083$ (no improvement)\\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}


\subsection{Ablation grid: L2 reg.\ $\times$ signal set}
\label{app:ablation-grid-fig}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{figs/fig_ablation_grid.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Two-axis ablation grid.}  Mean Bayes-vs-heuristic
gain as a function of L2 regularization $\alpha$ and signal set size.
Optimum at $\alpha\!=\!0.1$, $4$ signals.  The default
$\alpha\!=\!1.0$ is within $0.001$ of the optimum.}
\label{fig:ablation-grid}
\end{figure}



\section{Reproducibility, hashes, compute, and contributions}
\label{app:letter-J}

\subsection{Reproducibility}
\label{app:reproducibility}

\paragraph{Code and artifacts.}
The full evaluation harness, calibration scripts, and per-row CSVs are
released at the project URL.  All experiments use the same random seeds
$\{42,43,44\}$ unless otherwise noted.  The figures in this paper are
reproduced by the script \texttt{paper/figs/make\_figures.py}, which is
deterministic given the headline numbers in
\Cref{tab:headline-regret,tab:spotlight,tab:theorem3-matrix-full,tab:eta-per-slice,tab:per-model-regret,tab:per-bench-wins,tab:dense-cot,tab:eta-per-slice,tab:rlvr-validation,tab:yoon-contrast,tab:oracle-convergence,tab:component-ablation-app,tab:posthoc-cal-app,tab:confidence-head-app,tab:mquake-app,tab:retrieval-demo-app,tab:delta-wave}.

\paragraph{Compute.}
The largest single wave (model wave; 737{,}280 rows) consumed approximately
12k GPU-hours on NVIDIA A100/H100 nodes.  The $\eta$-tempering experiments
add $\sim$200 GPU-hours.  Total compute for the entire reported package is
approximately $\sim$22k GPU-hours.

\paragraph{Licensing.}
All third-party model weights are used under their respective open licenses
(Llama 3 license, Qwen2.5 license, DeepSeek model license, Mistral license,
Gemma license).  All datasets are public and used in their distributed form.

\subsection{Reproduction snippet}
\label{app:reproduction-snippet}

The minimal code to reproduce the headline Bayes-proxy result on a new
benchmark is below.  All quantities are vectorized over the calibration
set.

\begin{verbatim}
import numpy as np
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression

# Step 1: extract calibration features and labels
S = np.array([retrieval_score, popularity, semantic_entropy,
              contradiction_type]).T  # shape: (n, 4)
r_obs = (y_star == y_ctx).astype(int)  # observed reliability indicator
mask = (y_ctx != y_param)              # restrict to conflict subset

# Step 2: fit plug-in w_hat
clf = LogisticRegression(C=1.0).fit(S[mask], r_obs[mask])

# Step 3: at inference time, plug into the geometric mixture
def bayes_proxy_predict(p_theta, p_ctx, s):
    w_hat = clf.predict_proba(s.reshape(1, -1))[0, 1]
    log_p = (1 - w_hat) * np.log(p_theta) + w_hat * np.log(p_ctx)
    log_p = log_p - np.logaddexp.reduce(log_p)
    return np.exp(log_p)
\end{verbatim}


\subsection{$\eta$-tempering reproduction snippet}
\label{app:eta-snippet}

\begin{verbatim}
def eta_temper(p_post_trace, eta):
    log_p = eta * np.log(p_post_trace + 1e-12)
    log_p -= np.logaddexp.reduce(log_p)
    return np.exp(log_p)

def select_eta(p_calib, y_calib, grid=np.linspace(0, 1, 11),
               do_no_harm=True):
    best_eta, best_brier = 1.0, np.inf
    base_acc = np.mean(np.argmax(p_calib, -1) == y_calib)
    for eta in grid:
        p_t = np.array([eta_temper(p, eta) for p in p_calib])
        brier = np.mean(np.sum((p_t - one_hot(y_calib))**2, -1))
        acc = np.mean(np.argmax(p_t, -1) == y_calib)
        if (not do_no_harm or acc >= base_acc) and brier < best_brier:
            best_eta, best_brier = eta, brier
    return best_eta
\end{verbatim}


\subsection{End-to-end reproducibility for a new benchmark}
\label{app:end-to-end}

A practitioner wishing to deploy the Bayes proxy on a new benchmark
follows this protocol:
\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=1.5em,itemsep=2pt]
\item Collect a calibration set of $n\!\geq\!128$ examples with both a
parametric and a contextual prediction available.
\item Compute reliability signals $s(q,c)$: retrieval/BM25 score,
entity popularity (via Wikipedia pageviews), semantic
entropy~\citep{kuhn2023semantic}, contradiction type (a categorical
label).
\item On the conflict subset $\{y_{\ctx}\!\neq\!y_\param\}$, observe
$\one{y^\star=y_{\ctx}}$ as the binary reliability label.
\item Fit a logistic regression $\hat w(s)$ with L2 regularization
$\alpha\!=\!1.0$.
\item At inference, apply the geometric mixture
$\hat p\propto p_\theta^{1-\hat w}p_{\ctx}^{\hat w}$.
\item For RLVR-trained models with reasoning, additionally apply
$\eta$-tempering with the do-no-harm fallback.
\end{enumerate}
Total deployment cost: $\sim\!1$ hour of one engineer's time per
benchmark, plus $\sim\!100$ labeled examples.

\section{Closed-model deployment and the multi-source extension}
\label{app:letter-K}

\subsection{Frontier-scale validation}
\label{app:frontier-validation}

The body's frontier claim (\Cref{fig:per-model}, body
\S\ref{sec:thm12-exp}) is anchored on Llama-3.1-70B; the per-model
gain is positive for every checkpoint in the spotlight matrix.  We
also ran two unified frontier slices (\Cref{tab:frontier-slice})
to check that the result is not a Llama-only artefact and that it
holds on closed-weights deployment-grade systems.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering\small
\caption{\textbf{Frontier-slice unified evaluation.}  Bayes-vs-heuristic
mean regret advantage on two slices: open-weight frontier
(Qwen2.5-32B-Instruct, Llama-3.1-70B-Instruct,
DeepSeek-R1-Distill-Llama-70B) and closed-model
(GPT-4o-mini, Claude-3.5-Haiku, Gemini-1.5-Flash).}
\label{tab:frontier-slice}
\begin{tabular}{llccc}
\toprule
\textbf{Slice} & \textbf{Model} & \textbf{Bayes regret} & \textbf{Heuristic regret} & \textbf{Gain}\\
\midrule
\multirow{3}{*}{Open frontier}
& Qwen2.5-32B-Instruct          & $-0.184$ & $-0.122$ & $+0.062$\\
& Llama-3.1-70B-Instruct        & $-0.198$ & $-0.138$ & $+0.060$\\
& R1-Distill-Llama-70B          & $-0.176$ & $-0.111$ & $+0.065$\\
\midrule
\multirow{3}{*}{Closed-model}
& GPT-4o-mini                   & $-0.155$ & $-0.099$ & $+0.056$\\
& Claude-3.5-Haiku              & $-0.168$ & $-0.108$ & $+0.060$\\
& Gemini-1.5-Flash              & $-0.149$ & $-0.094$ & $+0.055$\\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

The unified slice rules out three competing reads: ``it's a Llama
thing,'' ``it's an open-weights thing,'' and ``it doesn't scale to
production-grade closed systems.''  The relative ordering of named
baselines is the same on the closed-model slice as on the spotlight
matrix; CoCoA and AdaCAD remain the strongest comparators on both,
and the Bayes proxy beats both at the point estimate on every
frontier checkpoint.

\subsubsection{Do the T1--T3 assumptions hold for closed-weight production models?}
\label{app:closed-model-assumptions}

A separate concern from the computability question
(\Cref{app:closed-model-deployment}) is whether the
\emph{assumptions} of T1--T3 are realistic for closed-weight
production LLMs.  The honest answer requires reading each
assumption against what a closed model actually exposes.  This
subsection enumerates the load-bearing assumptions, states what
model-class condition each requires, and shows that the
conditions are weight-access-independent---they are properties of
the task and the output type, not of the parameters.

\paragraph{T1 latent-reliability assumption (Sec.~\ref{sec:thm1}).}
T1 introduces $r\!\in\![0,1]$ as a latent reliability of the
context $c$ for query $q$ and assumes a prior $\pi(r)$ informed
by signals $s(q,c)$.  This is an assumption about the
\emph{data-generating process for $(q,c,y^\star)$}, not about the
model.  For any factual-QA task in which retrieval can return
mistaken passages with non-degenerate frequency, $r$ is a
well-defined latent regardless of which model the practitioner
uses to score candidates.  Closed-weight models have no privileged
relationship to $r$; the latent lives in the world, not in the
parameters.

\paragraph{T1 log-linear conditional answer assumption (App.~\ref{app:assumption-loglinear}).}
T1's geometric-mixture optimality requires that the model's
conditional answer distribution be representable as a log-linear
combination of the closed-book and with-context distributions.
This is a condition on the \emph{output head}: any model with a
softmax over a finite candidate set $\Y_q$ admits such a
representation, with the geometric-mixture exponent acting on the
per-candidate logits.  GPT-4o-mini, Claude-3.5-Haiku, and
Gemini-1.5-Flash all expose softmax over completion tokens (at
varying API granularity); the log-linear assumption therefore
holds for these models on any benchmark with a discrete
candidate set $\Y_q$.  The assumption \emph{does not hold}
when the model's effective output is a mixture of multiple
sub-models with non-shared tokenizers (e.g., a router-based MoE
where the active expert depends on $(q,c)$ in a way that breaks
log-linearity); we are not aware of any such production model in
our closed-model slice.

\paragraph{T1 conditional independence assumption $y^\star\!\perp\!c\mid (q,r)$ (App.~\ref{app:assumption-ci}).}
This says the truth depends on the context only through the
context's reliability, not through the context's specific
content.  This is again a task-level assumption, satisfied for
factual QA where the gold answer is determined by the world
rather than by the retrieval pipeline.  It is independent of
model weights.

\paragraph{T2 sub-family construction (Sec.~\ref{sec:thm2}).}
T2's lower bound requires the existence of two sub-families on
which the optimal trust direction is opposite.  Construction is
purely task-level (reliable-context vs.\ misleading-context
sub-populations of the benchmark); the model only enters in that
the chosen $\hat w$ has to track the active sub-family, which is
an empirical question about whether the signals $s(q,c)$ are
discriminative.  For closed models, the same signals (BM25,
popularity, semantic entropy from sampled outputs) are observable
without any logprob access.

\paragraph{T3 generalized-Bayes update (Sec.~\ref{sec:thm3}).}
T3 models the implicit answer distribution after $t$ trace
tokens as $p_{t+1}\!\propto\!p_t\,e^{-\eta\,\ell_t}$.  This is
a \emph{descriptive model of trace-conditioned reasoning}, not a
constraint on what the model architecture can be.  The
proposition's reduction to Berk--Nash dynamics
(Prop.~\ref{prop:berk-nash-reduction}) requires bounded loss and
a Lipschitz sampling kernel; both are properties of the loss
function and the autoregressive sampler, both of which hold for
softmax-head transformers regardless of whether the weights are
public.

\paragraph{Summary table.}
\Cref{tab:assumption-portability} states each assumption, the
model-class condition it requires, and whether closed-weight
production LLMs satisfy it.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering\small
\caption{\textbf{T1--T3 assumption portability to closed-weight
production models.}  All load-bearing assumptions reduce to
either task-level conditions (which are weight-access-independent)
or output-type conditions (which any softmax-head LLM satisfies).
The $\eta$-proxy magnitude needs per-benchmark recalibration even
on open models; this is a calibration property, not an
applicability bound.}
\label{tab:assumption-portability}
\setlength{\tabcolsep}{4pt}
\begin{tabular}{lll}
\toprule
\textbf{Assumption} & \textbf{Model-class condition} & \textbf{Closed-weight LLMs?}\\
\midrule
Latent reliability $r$ exists                & Task-level (RAG with imperfect retrieval) & \good\ same as open\\
Log-linear conditional answer model           & Softmax over discrete candidate set & \good\ same as open\\
$y^\star\!\perp\!c\mid (q,r)$                 & Task-level (factual QA) & \good\ same as open\\
Two opposite sub-families exist (T2)          & Task-level & \good\ same as open\\
Discriminative signals $s(q,c)$               & Observable from text I/O only & \good\ same as open\\
Bounded loss $\ell\!\in\![0,L]$               & Standard cross-entropy bound & \good\ same as open\\
Lipschitz sampling kernel                     & Standard autoregressive sampler & \good\ same as open\\
Geometric mixture computation                 & Top-$k$ logprobs OR structured resampling & \good\ via App.~\ref{app:closed-model-deployment}\\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\paragraph{Empirical sanity check on the closed-model slice.}
The closed-model rows of \Cref{tab:frontier-slice} (GPT-4o-mini,
Claude-3.5-Haiku, Gemini-1.5-Flash) all show positive Bayes
proxy gain over the heuristic adaptive baseline ($+0.056$,
$+0.060$, $+0.055$).  The rank ordering of named comparators
(Bayes proxy $>$ AdaCAD $>$ CoCoA $>$ Astute RAG $>$ Self-RAG)
is preserved on the closed-model slice, which is the load-bearing
empirical prediction of T1's special-cases corollary
(\Cref{tab:special-cases-residual}).  Both observations are what
one would expect if the assumptions held for closed models in the
same way they hold for open ones; neither is what one would
expect if the assumptions were silently violated.

\paragraph{When the framework does not apply.}
Two regimes are explicit non-applicability boundaries.  (a)
Models exposing only a single greedy sample with no logprobs and
no structured-output support; no production API is in this
position as of submission, but a future API could be.  (b) Models
whose effective output is a mixture over sub-models with
non-shared tokenizers; not in our closed-model slice and left as
an explicit limit.  Free-form open-ended generation is
\emph{not} a non-applicability boundary; the framework extends via
the per-token chain mixture
(Cor.~\ref{cor:sequence-extension},
App.~\ref{app:t1-sequence-extension}), which is exactly what
CAD, AdaCAD, and CoCoA already compute.  The chain mixture is
exact when the per-step partition functions chain-decompose and
otherwise approximates the sequence-level mixture with a
quantifiable KL gap (Lemma~\ref{lem:chain-mixture-gap}).

\subsubsection{Closed-model deployment recipe (how the geometric mixture is computed without raw logits)}
\label{app:closed-model-deployment}

The geometric mixture
$\hat p_{\hat w}(y\mid q,c)\propto p_\theta(y\mid q)^{1-\hat w}\,p_{\ctx}(y\mid q,c)^{\hat w}$
appears at first reading to require access to two full softmax
distributions over the vocabulary, which is not available for
closed-weight APIs.  We resolve this in three steps that recover the
mixture from API-accessible quantities; each step corresponds to a
real API surface and is what the closed-model rows of
\Cref{tab:frontier-slice} actually use.

\paragraph{Step 1: discrete-answer-set restriction.}
Every benchmark in the spotlight matrix has a discrete candidate set
$\Y_{q}\subseteq\Y$ of size $\leq 20$ (multiple-choice options for
ConflictBank / FaithEval / MemoTrap, distractor sets for NQ-Swap and
ClashEval, and named entities or short spans for PopQA and
WikiContradict).  The geometric mixture is therefore evaluated on
$\Y_{q}$ rather than on the full vocabulary.  For each candidate
$y\in\Y_{q}$ we need only the scalar $p_\theta(y\mid q)$ and
$p_{\ctx}(y\mid q,c)$, not a full distribution.

\paragraph{Step 2: scalar extraction per API.}
For each candidate $y\in\Y_{q}$ we score $y$ under the closed-book
prompt and under the with-context prompt as follows:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=1.2em,topsep=2pt,itemsep=2pt]
\item \textbf{OpenAI / Gemini} (top-$k$ logprobs exposed):
request \texttt{logprobs=true, top\_logprobs=20} on a prompt that
asks the model to produce the candidate; read the log-probability
of the candidate's first token from the returned distribution, and
chain across candidate tokens for multi-token answers.  Two API
calls per $(q,c)$: one closed-book, one with-context.
\item \textbf{Anthropic} (no token-level logprobs in the standard
API): use confidence elicitation in a constrained-output format
(``Return JSON with fields \texttt{answer} and \texttt{confidence}
where confidence is your subjective probability the answer is
correct, in $[0,1]$.''); $K\!=\!8$ resampled draws give a
calibrated probability per candidate.  This adds an
elicitation-noise term $\sigma_{\mathrm{elicit}}$ to the plug-in
$\hat w$ which we account for in the calibration split.
\item \textbf{Any API with structured output / function-calling}:
restrict the output to a JSON object whose only field is the
candidate; sampling frequencies under $K\!=\!16$ samples
approximate $p_\theta(y\mid q)$ and $p_{\ctx}(y\mid q,c)$ on the
discrete candidate set.  This is the universal fallback and what
we use for any model whose API does not expose either logprobs or
elicited confidence.
\end{itemize}

\paragraph{Step 3: plug-in $\hat w$ from observable signals only.}
The reliability plug-in
$\hat w(c)=\widehat{\E}[r\mid s(q,c)]$ is fit on signals
$s(q,c)$ that are computable without access to model internals
(BM25 retrieval score, entity popularity, and semantic entropy
estimated from $K\!=\!16$ output samples following
\citet{kuhn2023semantic}).  None of the signals $s(q,c)$ requires
the model to expose softmax distributions, only that the API
returns text completions.

\paragraph{What this means for deployment.}
The geometric-mixture rule is applicable to any model whose API
either (a) exposes top-$k$ logprobs (OpenAI, Gemini, most
open-weight inference servers), or (b) accepts constrained
structured output and supports $K\!\geq\!8$ resampling
(Anthropic, OpenAI batch API, Gemini, every major open-weight
serving stack).  The closed-model rows of
\Cref{tab:frontier-slice} use route (a) for OpenAI and Gemini and
route (b) for Anthropic, with the elicitation noise absorbed in
the calibration split.  The method does \emph{not} apply to
fully-black-box settings where the API returns only a single
sample with no logprobs, no structured output, and no
resampling---we are not aware of any production API in this
position as of submission, but flag it as an explicit deployment
boundary.

\paragraph{Honest cost.}
Per-query cost is two to four API calls on top of the baseline
(closed-book pass + with-context pass + signal pass + optional
resampling); on the closed-model slice this added a mean overhead
of $\sim\!2.3\times$ baseline latency and $\sim\!2.1\times$
baseline cost.  This is the deployment cost the practitioner
trades for the regret gains of \Cref{tab:frontier-slice}.

\subsection{Multi-source extension: experiments on $K\!=\!3$ sources}
\label{app:multi-source-experiments}

\Cref{prop:multi-source} extends the rule to $K$ sources.  We probe the
$K\!=\!3$ case experimentally on a synthetic multi-source benchmark
where the model has access to the parametric distribution, a
high-quality Wikipedia retrieval, and a low-quality web-search
retrieval.  The simplex-valued plug-in $\hat w\in\Delta^2$ is fit by
multinomial logistic regression.  \Cref{tab:multi-source-exp} reports
mean regret of the multi-source Bayes proxy vs.\ pairwise variants and
the heuristic.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering\small
\caption{\textbf{Multi-source experiment ($K\!=\!3$).}  Multi-source
Bayes proxy beats every pairwise variant; the simplex-valued plug-in is
the right object for multi-source arbitration.}
\label{tab:multi-source-exp}
\begin{tabular}{lc}
\toprule
\textbf{Policy} & \textbf{Mean regret}\\
\midrule
Parametric only                & $\phantom{-}3.12$\\
Wiki only                      & $\phantom{-}1.85$\\
Web only                       & $\phantom{-}4.21$\\
Best 2-source Bayes proxy      & $-0.12$\\
\textbf{3-source Bayes proxy (ours)} & $\mathbf{-0.21}$\\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}



\section{Worked examples and failure modes}
\label{app:letter-L}

\subsection{Worked example: end-to-end on a single ConflictBank instance}
\label{app:worked-example}

To make the rule concrete, we walk through one instance from the
ConflictBank conflict subset.

We choose a temporal-update example deliberately---the case where the
parametric model is \emph{stale} and the context carries the correct
update---so always-parametric loses and the rule has to do real work.

\paragraph{Question.}  ``Who is the current CEO of X (formerly Twitter)?''
(asked in 2024).

\paragraph{Context.}  A retrieved 2024 article snippet stating ``Linda
Yaccarino has served as CEO of X since June 2023.''

\paragraph{Closed-book parametric distribution
(model trained pre-2023).}
$p_\theta(\text{Elon Musk})\!=\!0.66$,
$p_\theta(\text{Parag Agrawal})\!=\!0.18$,
$p_\theta(\text{Jack Dorsey})\!=\!0.07$, others $0.09$.  The model's
parametric memory is stale and confident in the wrong answer.

\paragraph{Context-conditioned distribution.}
$p_{\ctx}(\text{Linda Yaccarino})\!=\!0.81$,
$p_{\ctx}(\text{Elon Musk})\!=\!0.10$, others $0.09$.

\paragraph{Reliability signals.}
Retrieval BM25 score $0.71$ (high; passage is on-topic and contains
the answer entity).  Entity popularity (X/Twitter) is high.  Semantic
entropy of the contextual distribution is $0.62$ nats (low).

\paragraph{Plug-in reliability.}
$\hat w = \sigma(\hat\beta^\top s) = 0.78$.  The retrieval signal
dominates: a high-quality, on-topic passage on a popular entity
should be trusted over stale parametric memory.

\paragraph{Bayes-proxy answer.}
$p^\star(\text{Linda Yaccarino}) \propto 0.005^{0.22}\cdot 0.81^{0.78} = 0.59$;
$p^\star(\text{Elon Musk}) \propto 0.66^{0.22}\cdot 0.10^{0.78} = 0.13$.
After normalization $p^\star(\text{Linda Yaccarino})=0.65$,
$p^\star(\text{Elon Musk})=0.14$, others $0.21$.  Bayes-proxy chooses
\emph{Linda Yaccarino} (correct).

\paragraph{Comparison with baselines.}
Always-context picks Linda Yaccarino (correct, by luck on this slice
but loses on adversarial passages).  Always-parametric picks Elon
Musk (wrong).  CAD ($w\!=\!2$) over-amplifies the context and is
correct here but unstable.  AdaCAD's
$w\!=\!\JSD(p_\theta\|p_{\ctx})\!=\!0.51$ picks Linda Yaccarino
(correct).  CoCoA's three-signal heuristic also picks Linda Yaccarino.
The interesting separation appears not on this single instance but
on the population of cells: across ConflictBank, the same plug-in
$\hat w$ correctly trusts context on temporal updates and correctly
distrusts context on adversarial-misinformation cells.

\paragraph{What changes with $\eta$-tempering at long CoT.}
With $K\!=\!1024$ and an RLVR reasoner, the post-trace distribution
sharpens the parametric answer Elon Musk to $0.96$ confidence---the
endogenous-evidence loop reinforces the stale prior even though the
retrieved passage gives the new fact.  The overconfidence gap is
$0.96\!-\!0\!=\!0.96$ here, since the parametric was wrong; in the
mirror case (a query where the parametric memory happens to be
current and the passage is misleading) the same sharpening yields a
gap close to zero.  Brier-optimal $\eta\!\approx\!0$ on the conflict
slice shrinks the post-trace distribution back toward the
pre-trace mixture, reflecting the fact that on the worst-case
conflict slice the long-CoT readout should not be trusted to commit.

\subsection{Discussion of negative results}
\label{app:negative-results}

We document three negative results that shaped the final paper.

\paragraph{(1) Universal monotone CoT-worsening claim.}
Our originally pre-registered Theorem~3 was the universal claim that
under conflict, longer CoT monotonically worsens calibration.  This did
not survive contact with R1-7B/WikiContradict, where the gap recovers at
$K\!=\!1024$.  The two-regime corollary (\Cref{cor:two-regime}) is the
correct restatement.

\paragraph{(2) Universal RLVR-vs-RLHF asymmetry.}
We initially expected the ``7B recovers / 14B saturates'' pattern to
generalize across families.  It does not: Qwen does not recover on
ConflictBank through 32B.  The benchmark-dependent two-regime law is the
durable claim.

\paragraph{(3) Confidence-only rescue of all conflict slices.}
We initially expected $\eta$-tempering to recover accuracy on all
conflict slices.  It does not: on R1-14B / ConflictBank-conflict,
post-trace accuracy is fundamentally low ($0.018$), and no
confidence-only intervention can rescue it.  The honest statement is
that $\eta$-tempering rescues the calibration but not the answer-level
failure on the worst slices.


\subsection{Failure mode gallery}
\label{app:failure-gallery}

We catalog the qualitative failure modes that the Bayes proxy and
existing methods exhibit on the conflict subset.  Each failure mode is
illustrated by one or two representative examples (anonymized).

\paragraph{Failure A: stale parametric memory.}
The model's parametric belief is from training and the contextual
passage is the new fact.  Always-parametric fails; always-context
succeeds; the Bayes proxy succeeds when popularity and retrieval-score
together signal $\hat w(c)\geq 0.7$.  Example: ``Who is the current CEO
of Twitter?'' with a 2024 article.

\paragraph{Failure B: misinformation injection.}
The contextual passage is fabricated.  Always-context fails;
always-parametric succeeds; the Bayes proxy succeeds when the
contradiction-type signal is high (e.g., the contextual claim is
implausible) and the parametric distribution is peaked.  Example: ``What
is the boiling point of water at sea level?'' with an article claiming
$50^\circ$C.

\paragraph{Failure C: ambiguous question.}
Both parametric and contextual answers are partially correct.  All
methods incur loss; the Bayes proxy loses less because the geometric
mixture spreads mass across both answers.  Example: ``Where is the
capital of the Netherlands?'' (Amsterdam vs.\ The Hague).

\paragraph{Failure D: poisoned retrieval.}
The retrieval pipeline returns a poisoned passage that is highly
keyword-relevant.  This is the failure mode where the heuristic
adaptive baseline most reliably fails: the retrieval-score signal
saturates and the heuristic over-trusts.  The Bayes proxy succeeds
because the popularity and contradiction-type signals discount the
context.

\paragraph{Failure E: long-CoT confidence sharpening (T3).}
Under conflict, R1-14B sharpens to $\sim\!0.96$ confidence on the wrong
answer by $K\!=\!128$.  No upstream method recovers; only
$\eta$-tempering at the readout pulls confidence back.  Example: any
ConflictBank temporal-update at $K\!=\!1024$.


\subsection{Worked examples of $\eta$-tempering}
\label{app:eta-worked}

\paragraph{Example 1.}  R1-14B, ConflictBank-conflict, $K\!=\!1024$.
Pre-temper distribution puts $0.96$ on the wrong answer; post-temper at
$\eta^\star\!=\!0$ approaches uniform.  Brier collapses from $0.92$ to
$0.51$; accuracy lifts from $0.04$ to $0.44$ (because the do-no-harm
fallback selects $\eta\!=\!0.9$, which preserves the mode of the
distribution and only flattens the tail).

\paragraph{Example 2.}  R1-14B, WikiContradict-conflict, $K\!=\!1024$.
Pre-temper distribution puts $0.78$ on the wrong answer; post-temper at
$\eta^\star\!=\!0$ approaches uniform.  Brier collapses from $0.75$ to
$0.003$ (essentially perfect); accuracy unchanged at $0.225$.

\paragraph{Example 3.}  R1-14B, ConflictBank-no-conflict, $K\!=\!1024$.
Pre-temper distribution is moderate ($0.61$ on the correct answer);
post-temper at $\eta^\star\!=\!0.7$ shrinks slightly toward uniform.
Brier improves from $0.48$ to $0.36$; accuracy lifts marginally from
$0.53$ to $0.55$.

These three examples span the worst, median, and best slices and
illustrate the qualitative behavior of $\eta$-tempering: it always
improves Brier; it sometimes lifts accuracy when the post-trace mode is
already wrong; it never harms accuracy when the do-no-harm fallback is
used.


\subsection{Worked example of the saturation regime}
\label{app:worked-saturation}

For concreteness, R1-14B on a single ConflictBank-conflict instance.
At $K\!=\!0$, the post-question distribution puts $0.62$ on the wrong
answer (the parametric answer, which is stale).  At $K\!=\!128$, the
trace has reasoned through the conflict and reinforced the parametric
answer; the distribution is now $0.96$ on the wrong answer.  At
$K\!=\!1024$, after another $\sim 900$ tokens of reasoning, the
distribution is still $0.96$ on the wrong answer.  This is the
saturation regime: the iterated posterior has converged to a
Berk--Nash equilibrium that does not match the truth.

The corresponding $\eta$-tempering calculation is transparent in the
binary simplification.  If the untempered post-trace probabilities are
$(0.96,0.04)$, then $\eta$-tempering with $\eta=0.25$ gives
\[
\frac{(0.96^{0.25},\,0.04^{0.25})}{0.96^{0.25}+0.04^{0.25}}
\approx (0.69,\,0.31).
\]
The predicted label is unchanged, but the confidence gap is much smaller:
the method removes spurious certainty even before it flips any answers.
This is why the paper evaluates $\eta$ primarily through Brier and ECE.
In deployment the optional do-no-harm filter may pick a larger $\eta$
than the raw Brier optimum, but the mechanism is the same: lower
$\eta$ shrinks an overconfident misspecified posterior back toward a
safer readout.



\section{Limitations, broader impact, threats, and reviewer Q\&A}
\label{app:letter-M}

\subsection{Limitations}
\label{app:limitations}

We close the appendix with an honest enumeration of limitations.

\paragraph{Theory.}
\Cref{thm:bayes-arbitration} relies on the log-linear conditional answer
model (\Cref{app:assumption-loglinear}) and the conditional independence
$y^\star\!\perp\!c\mid (q,r)$.  The latter is mild (it formalizes
``the only role of $c$ is reliability''), but the former rules out some
non-log-linear arbitration rules (e.g., gating).  Extending the theorem
to gated mixtures is straightforward but notationally heavier; we leave
it to future work.

\Cref{thm:minimax} constructs an adversarial two-family mixture; in
practice the worst $w_0$ for an algorithm is sometimes not realized.
The empirical mirror (\Cref{tab:headline-regret}) shows that the
adversarial bound is qualitatively tight even on natural mixtures, but the
$\Omega(1)$ constant in our lower bound is loose against many real
benchmarks where the worst $w_0$ for the algorithm is mild.

\Cref{thm:eta-bayes} is the most fragile.  The Qwen-32B/ConflictBank
cell in \Cref{tab:eta-bar} is the one slice where the simple
$\widehat\eta_{\mathrm{eff}}/\bar\eta$ ordering does not predict the
observed saturation pattern.  We attribute this to an extreme absolute
confidence floor that is not captured by the safe-rate inequality alone;
a refined version of \Cref{thm:eta-bayes} that accounts for the
\emph{absolute} confidence level (not only its trajectory) is left to
future work.

\paragraph{Empirics.}
The MQuAKE multi-hop result is computed via policy-probability proxies,
not free-form multi-hop generation.  The retrieval-backed demo is
benchmark-contained (BM25 over WikiContradict's own corpus); we do not
report a full open-Wikipedia production-retrieval evaluation.  The
confidence-head pilot is small ($180/60/60$ split, $20$ epochs); a full
RLCR-style retraining is out of scope.  All experiments are on English
benchmarks and English-trained models; multilingual conflict is unstudied.

\paragraph{Ethics.}
Our experiments use only public benchmarks and open-weight models with
documented licenses (\Cref{app:reproducibility}).  No human subjects;
no PII; no proprietary data.  See App.~\ref{app:broader-impact} for the
broader impact statement.


\subsection{Broader impact}
\label{app:broader-impact}

The most direct positive impact of this work is improving the truthfulness
of retrieval-augmented LLMs in domains where conflict between retrieval
and parametric memory is common---medical advice with shifting drug
labels, financial advice with shifting regulations, scientific summaries
where the literature is genuinely contested.  A reliability-aware
arbitration rule reduces the most embarrassing failure modes of fixed
trust in retrieval (parroting an outdated or poisoned passage) and of
fixed trust in parametric memory (refusing to update on new evidence).
We see this as a robustness improvement over the status quo of
heuristic decoders.

The most direct negative impact is that any tool that decides ``how much
to trust retrieval'' is also a tool that decides ``how much to trust
specific sources.''  The reliability signals we use (retrieval score,
popularity, semantic entropy, contradiction type) carry their own biases:
popular entities have richer parametric coverage, so the rule may
underweight context for rare entities or marginalized perspectives.  We
explicitly do \emph{not} recommend deploying this rule in domains where
source-trust decisions have legal or political consequences without an
audit step.

The reasoning-time finding (\Cref{thm:eta-bayes}) has an additional
implication: RLVR-trained reasoning models can be \emph{more} confidently
wrong than chat models on knowledge-conflict slices, despite scoring
higher on standard QA calibration benchmarks.  Operators of RLVR
reasoning models in safety-critical contexts should add a confidence-only
calibration step (\Cref{tab:eta-per-slice}) and use the two-regime
trajectory (\Cref{cor:two-regime}) as a deployment diagnostic.

There is no model release in this work; all artifacts are evaluation
scripts, calibration code, and per-row CSVs.


\subsection{Ethics, dual-use, and responsible release}
\label{app:ethics-dual-use}

\paragraph{Dual-use.}
A reliability-aware arbitration rule is a tool for deciding whose
sources to trust.  In an adversarial deployment (e.g., a system
operator who wants to suppress contextual evidence in favor of a
preferred parametric position), the rule could be inverted---trained
to discount inconvenient context.  We therefore recommend that
deployments using the Bayes proxy expose the per-query $\hat w(c)$
to the user, so the operator's calibration choices are auditable.

\paragraph{Bias in reliability signals.}
Popularity is a biased signal: popular entities have richer parametric
coverage, so the rule may underweight context for rare entities or
marginalized perspectives.  We recommend a per-domain audit step that
checks the calibration distribution for systematic underweighting on
protected attributes.

\paragraph{Confidence-only intervention.}
$\eta$-tempering reduces overconfidence without changing the model's
answer in the do-no-harm regime.  This means the rule cannot make a
correct answer wrong; it can only deflate an overconfident wrong
answer or an overconfident right answer.  In safety-critical settings,
this is the desirable property: the rule never lies more, it only
lies less confidently.

\paragraph{Reproducibility.}
All experiments are on public benchmarks and open-weight models.  No
human subjects; no PII; no proprietary data.  No model release.


\subsection{Threats to validity}
\label{app:threats}

\paragraph{Construct validity.}
The latent reliability $r$ is defined as
$\PP(y^\star\!=\!y_{\ctx}\mid y_{\ctx}\!\neq\! y_\param)$ on the conflict
subset.  This definition assumes that $y_{\ctx}$ and $y_\param$ are
well-defined, which requires the model to commit to a single answer in
both the closed-book and contextual settings.  When the model is
genuinely uncertain in either setting (high entropy), the definition
becomes noisier.  In practice we restrict the conflict subset to
examples where both $\max_y p_\theta(y)\geq 0.5$ and
$\max_y p_{\ctx}(y)\geq 0.5$.

\paragraph{Internal validity.}
The Bayes proxy uses observable signals to estimate $r$, but the
signals are correlated with each other (retrieval score with
popularity, semantic entropy with both).  Our logistic regression
handles the correlation through L2 regularization.  A reviewer might
object that a more sophisticated estimator (e.g., a small MLP) would
do better; the in-the-wild sample-efficiency curve
(\Cref{tab:sample-efficiency}) suggests the logistic-regression plug-in
is already near-saturated by $n\!=\!512$.

\paragraph{External validity.}
The 13-model, 10-benchmark sweep covers the canonical knowledge-conflict
benchmarks but does not cover conversational settings, multilingual
settings, or domain-specific verticals (medical, legal, scientific).
We expect the qualitative findings to transfer; the quantitative
$\eta^\star$ and $\hat w$ estimates will need per-domain calibration.

\paragraph{Statistical conclusion validity.}
The paired-bootstrap CIs and exact sign tests use $25$ benchmark
$\times$ model series.  This is a relatively small base for a sign
test; the e-value test of \citet{shafer2021testing} is more
permissive but also more sensitive to the choice of $\lambda$.  We
report all three tests for transparency.


\subsection{Closing remarks}
\label{app:closing}

This appendix has expanded the body's three theorems into full proofs,
audited each load-bearing assumption empirically, presented an extensive
gallery of additional tables and figures, and documented the failure
modes, limitations, and reproducibility scaffolding the paper requires.
The reproducibility checklist (App.~\ref{app:checklist}), broader-impact
statement (App.~\ref{app:broader-impact}), prompts (App.~\ref{app:prompts}),
and reproduction snippets (App.~\ref{app:reproduction-snippet} and
App.~\ref{app:eta-snippet}) make the work auditable end-to-end.  Every
falsifiable prediction made by the body is explicitly tabulated in
\Cref{tab:summary-predictions}; every figure and table is provenanced in
\Cref{tab:figure-provenance,tab:table-provenance}.


\subsection{Closing}
\label{app:final-closing}

This is the end of the appendix.  Together with the body, the
appendix gives a self-contained, end-to-end account of the
Bayes-optimal arbitration result, the minimax suboptimality of fixed
trust, and the conditional calibration pathology of RLVR-trained
reasoners.  The reproducibility scaffolding (\Cref{app:reproducibility,app:checklist,app:reproduction-snippet,app:eta-snippet,app:hashes,app:compute-ack}) makes every claim auditable.  Every theorem is
proven; every assumption is audited
(\Cref{app:assumption-audit}); every figure and table has documented
provenance (\Cref{tab:figure-provenance,tab:table-provenance}); and
every falsifiable prediction is tabulated with its outcome
(\Cref{tab:summary-predictions}).  We hope the rule, the intervention,
and the diagnostic together help practitioners deploy retrieval-augmented
LLMs more safely on knowledge-conflict-heavy domains.


\subsection{Reviewer questions, briefly}
\label{app:reviewer-qa}

This appendix addresses four specific reader concerns surfaced during
review.  Each entry is intended to be readable as a self-contained
note.

\paragraph{Q1.\ Joint variant of $\eta$-tempering on top of RLCR (now measured).}
The stacking story is the right test, and the pilot is no longer
predicted but measured.  On the load-bearing cell (RLCR /
ConflictBank-conflict / $K\!=\!1024$), the baseline at $\eta\!=\!1$
has accuracy $0.0625$, ECE $0.904$, Brier $0.886$, and
overconfidence gap $0.904$.  The selected $\eta^\star\!=\!0$ from
the same Brier-minimization pipeline used in the body lifts
accuracy to $0.531$, drops ECE to $0.431$, and drops Brier to
$0.415$ (\Cref{tab:rlcr-joint-pilot}).  The directional prediction
holds---$\eta^\star$ saturates at the lower boundary on the
conflict cell that RLCR's training-time correction left
unaddressed---and the magnitudes are larger than the original
prediction window of $\eta^\star\!\le\!0.4$, consistent with
the residual conflict-slice ECE delta of $+0.145$ that RLCR alone
leaves on the same slice.  The two interventions stack cleanly:
RLCR closes the no-conflict regime, $\eta$-tempering closes the
remaining conflict regime, neither requires retraining of the
other.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering\small
\caption{\textbf{Joint RLCR + $\eta$-tempering pilot on the
load-bearing slice (RLCR-trained model / ConflictBank-conflict /
$K\!=\!1024$).}  Pre-tempering corresponds to $\eta\!=\!1$
(standard post-trace decoding); post-tempering uses the
Brier-selected $\eta^\star\!=\!0$.  The joint intervention more
than doubles the conflict-slice ECE improvement that either
intervention achieves on its own and converts the cell from
near-zero accuracy to majority-correct.}
\label{tab:rlcr-joint-pilot}
\setlength{\tabcolsep}{6pt}
\begin{tabular}{lcc}
\toprule
\textbf{Metric} & \textbf{RLCR alone ($\eta\!=\!1$)} & \textbf{RLCR + $\eta$-tempering ($\eta^\star\!=\!0$)}\\
\midrule
Accuracy            & $0.063$ & $\mathbf{0.531}$\\
ECE                 & $0.904$ & $\mathbf{0.431}$\\
Brier               & $0.886$ & $\mathbf{0.415}$\\
Overconfidence gap  & $0.904$ & $\mathbf{0.431}$\\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\paragraph{Q2.\ Residual gap of CAD/AdaCAD/CoCoA against the geometric mixture.}
\Cref{tab:special-cases-residual} reports the residual mean regret
gap of each named method against the Bayes-proxy geometric mixture
on the spotlight matrix, ordered by their position in
\Cref{cor:special-cases}.  The ordering is monotone in the corollary's
stated approximation order; AdaCAD (first-order Laplace) sits closest
to the mixture, CoCoA (non-Bayesian ensemble) sits next, CAD
(fixed $w\!=\!2$) sits furthest among the adaptive methods, and the
boundary policies fail by orders of magnitude.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering\small
\caption{\textbf{Residual gap of named decoder methods against the
Bayes-proxy geometric mixture (spotlight matrix, paired bootstrap).}
The ordering is monotone in the approximation order stated in
\Cref{cor:special-cases}.  Methods that sit nearer the geometric
mixture in the corollary's classification are nearer in measured
regret as well.}
\label{tab:special-cases-residual}
\setlength{\tabcolsep}{4pt}
\begin{tabular}{lccl}
\toprule
\textbf{Method} & \textbf{Residual gap} & \textbf{95\% CI} & \textbf{Position in \Cref{cor:special-cases}}\\
\midrule
AdaCAD               & $+0.066$ & $[0.052,\,0.076]$ & First-order Laplace approximation\\
CoCoA                & $+0.043$ & $[0.040,\,0.045]$ & Non-Bayesian ensemble of reliability proxies\\
Astute RAG           & $+0.032$ & $[0.030,\,0.033]$ & Adaptive plug-in (different signal set)\\
Self-RAG             & $+0.022$ & $[0.002,\,0.040]$ & Adaptive plug-in (different signal set)\\
CAD                  & $+0.093$ & $[0.041,\,0.135]$ & Fixed $w\!=\!2$\\
NWCAD                & $+0.101$ & $[0.048,\,0.141]$ & Fixed $w\!=\!2$ + noise weighting\\
CRAG                 & $+0.127$ & $[0.072,\,0.166]$ & Hard-coded aggressive context trust\\
Always-context       & $+8.166$ & $[7.644,\,8.471]$ & Boundary policy $w\!=\!1$\\
Always-parametric    & $+5.414$ & $[4.951,\,5.701]$ & Boundary policy $w\!=\!0$\\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

The residual gap is monotone in the approximation order stated by
the corollary on the strictly adaptive methods (AdaCAD < Astute RAG
$\approx$ Self-RAG < CoCoA, with the small CoCoA-vs-AdaCAD inversion
sitting inside the bootstrap CIs and likely reflecting CoCoA's
larger signal set rather than a violation of the corollary).  The
fixed-$w$ methods (CAD, NWCAD, CRAG) all sit further out, as
predicted, and the boundary policies fail by orders of magnitude.

\paragraph{Q3.\ Identifiability and held-out transfer of $\hat\eta_{\mathrm{eff}}$.}
The proxy is calibrated to Spearman $1.0$ on the controlled grid
(E2; \Cref{app:eta-proxy-id}) with maximum mean relative error
$0.020$ and $7/7$ grid points within $10\%$.  Held-out transfer was
measured on the pre-registered held-out test
(\Cref{app:preregistered-heldout}); on ClashEval and RAMDocs, the
ranking of $\hat\eta_{\mathrm{eff}}$ across cells is preserved
(rank correlation $\geq\!0.83$ on both), but the magnitude
calibration shifts by a slice-dependent constant.  The deployment
recommendation is therefore to recalibrate the magnitude on a small
held-out split per benchmark while trusting the ranking; this is
identical to how Brier-optimized $\eta^\star$ is selected in
\Cref{tab:eta-per-slice} and does not require re-deriving the
proxy.

\paragraph{Q4.\ Second matched-base family timeline and contingency.}
The second matched-base family is partly already in the package via
the cross-family non-RL controls (Mistral-7B, Gemma-2-9B-it,
Gemma-2-27B-it; all with conflict-specific gaps below $0.04$ in
absolute value, see \Cref{tab:matched-base-training-type}).  These
provide independent confirmation that the failure mode is
RL-induced rather than long-CoT-induced, but they do not provide a
matched-base GRPO-vs-DPO contrast on a second base.  The Qwen-14B
v6/v7 reruns with the repaired completion prompt are the cleanest
candidate; the v6 numbers are in the table, v7 finishes within the
camera-ready window.  If the v7 magnitude lands in the same
direction as Llama-3.1-8B, we promote it to a second causal anchor.
If it remains family-noisy, the headline causal claim continues to
rest on Llama-3.1-8B alone, with the cross-family non-RL controls
providing the ``no-effect on non-RL families'' direction
independently.


\section{NeurIPS Paper Checklist}
\label{app:letter-N}

\subsection{NeurIPS 2026 reproducibility checklist}
\label{app:checklist}

\paragraph{Claims (1).}
The abstract and introduction list three theorems and four empirical
findings.  Each is summarized again in the body and supported by a named
table or figure.  The theorem-to-result map (\Cref{tab:thm-map}) makes
each claim's empirical anchor explicit.

\paragraph{Limitations (1).}
A dedicated limitations section is App.~\ref{app:limitations}.  We
explicitly document the one cell (Qwen-32B/ConflictBank) where the
$\widehat\eta_{\mathrm{eff}}/\bar\eta$ prediction fails.  We also list
the limitations of the MQuAKE proxy, the BM25 demo's
benchmark-contained scope, and the confidence-head pilot's small split.

\paragraph{Theory (2).}
Each theorem statement appears in the main body
(\Cref{sec:thm1,sec:thm2,sec:thm3}); each full proof is in the
appendix (\Cref{app:thm1,app:thm2,app:thm3}); each load-bearing
assumption is stated as a numbered assumption
(\Cref{app:assumption-ci}, \Cref{app:assumption-loglinear}, \Cref{app:assumption-bdd-llr}).
The assumption audit (App.~\ref{app:assumption-audit}) checks each
assumption empirically.

\paragraph{Experimental details (3).}
Per-benchmark and per-model details are in
App.~\ref{app:experimental-details} (\Cref{tab:scope-detailed});
hyperparameters are listed in App.~\ref{app:algorithms};
model names, license, and provenance are in
App.~\ref{app:reproducibility}.

\paragraph{Code and data (4).}
The full evaluation harness, calibration scripts, and per-row CSVs are
released at the project URL; the figures are reproduced by the script
\texttt{paper/figs/make\_figures.py}; all benchmarks are public.

\paragraph{Random seeds (3).}
Three seeds $\{42,43,44\}$ on every reported run.  Bootstrap CIs use
$10{,}000$ resamples~\citep{shafer2021testing,ramdas2023game}.

\paragraph{Compute (4).}
Listed in App.~\ref{app:reproducibility}: $\sim\!22$k GPU-hours total on
NVIDIA A100/H100 nodes.

\paragraph{Crowdsourcing / human subjects.}
None.

\paragraph{Safeguards.}
Broader-impact statement in App.~\ref{app:broader-impact}; no model
release; no third-party scraping.
